Given N, the proper divisors are all numbers d such that d|N and d<N. The mapping d -> N/d is a bijection between divisors and divisors, but it's not necessarily order-preserving. The largest proper divisor is N/p where p is the smallest prime factor. The second largest is either N/p^2 or N/q depending on the prime factorization. The third largest is the next largest among the remaining candidates. For f(N)=N, we need sum of these three largest equals N.

We already solved for two-prime case and got fixed points only of the form 2*3^b. For three-prime case, we need to find all N such that the three largest proper divisors sum to N. Let's analyze the condition for numbers with three distinct primes.

Let N = p^a q^b r^c with p<q<r. The largest proper divisor is N/p = p^{a-1} q^b r^c. The second largest: compare N/p^2 (if a≥2) and N/q = p^a q^{b-1} r^c. Since p<q, N/q < N/p. Also N/p^2 = p^{a-2} q^b r^c. Which is larger? Their ratio: (N/p^2) / (N/q) = q / p^2. So if q > p^2, then N/p^2 > N/q; if q < p^2, then N/p^2 < N/q; if q = p^2, then equal. Since p is smallest prime, p=2 or 3 etc. For p=2, q>4? Then q≥5, so q>p^2=4, so N/p^2 > N/q. For p=3, p^2=9, q>3, q could be 5,7,11,... So if q<9, i.e., q=5 or 7, then q<p^2, so N/p^2 < N/q. If q>9, i.e., q=11,13,..., then q>p^2, so N/p^2 > N/q. So the second largest is either N/p^2 or N/q.

Similarly, the third largest will be the next largest among the remaining candidates. The candidates for the third largest: if a≥3, then N/p^3 = p^{a-3} q^b r^c; if a≥2 and b≥1, then N/(p q) = p^{a-1} q^{b-1} r^c; if a≥2 and c≥1, then N/(p r) = p^{a-1} q^b r^{c-1}; if b≥2, then N/q^2 = p^a q^{b-2} r^c; if c≥2, then N/r^2 = p^a q^b r^{c-2}; also N/(p^2 q)? etc. Also possibly N/(p q r)? That's N/(pqr) which is probably small. So the third largest will be the maximum among these that is less than the second largest.

Let N have at least 3 proper divisors. Write the three largest proper divisors as d1 > d2 > d3. Let N be any composite number with at least three proper divisors. Consider its set of divisors. The three largest proper divisors are the three largest numbers among divisors less than N. Since divisors come in complementary pairs, the largest proper divisor is N/p. The second largest is either N/p^2 or N/q. The third largest is then the maximum among the remaining divisors that are less than the second largest.

Let's denote s1 = N/p, s2 = max(N/p^2, N/q), s3 = the third largest. Then s1 + s2 + s3 = N.

We can try to find all N satisfying this.

But the problem asks for all possible values of a_1 such that the infinite sequence exists. That is, we need to find all a_1 for which the sequence defined by a_{n+1}=f(a_n) is defined for all n (i.e., each a_n has at least 3 proper divisors). We are not asked to find all a_1 for which the sequence is defined; we are asked to determine all possible values of a_1. That is, we need to find all positive integers a_1 such that the sequence a_1, a_2, ... consists of positive integers, each of which has at least three proper divisors. So a_1 itself must have at least three proper divisors. Also, we need to ensure that the recursion never produces a number with fewer than three proper divisors. So we need to characterize the set of starting numbers that yield an infinite sequence.

We have found that numbers N such that f(N)=N (fixed points) work, because the sequence becomes constant and all terms are fixed points, which have at least three proper divisors. Also, numbers that eventually reach a fixed point also work, provided that all intermediate terms have at least three proper divisors. So we need to find all a_1 such that the orbit under f never hits a number with <3 proper divisors. Since f is defined only for numbers with at least 3 proper divisors, we need to avoid numbers with exactly 0,1,2 proper divisors. So we need to know which numbers are "safe" (i.e., have at least 3 proper divisors) and which are "unsafe" (have fewer than 3 proper divisors). The "unsafe" numbers are: 1 (0 proper divisors), primes (1 proper divisor), and squares of primes (2 proper divisors). Also, numbers like p^3 have 3 proper divisors, so they are safe. So the condition for a number to be safe is that it is not prime and not a prime square. Also, note that 1 is not allowed. So the safe numbers are composites that are not prime powers with exponent 2? Actually, p^3 is safe (3 proper divisors). p^4 has proper divisors 1,p,p^2,p^3? That includes numbers with at least 4 divisors total, i.e., composite numbers that are not prime or prime square. So safe numbers are all composite numbers except p^2 for prime p. Also, note that p^2 has exactly 2 proper divisors (1 and p). So they are unsafe. Also, primes are unsafe. 1 is unsafe.

So we need to find all a_1 such that the orbit stays within the set of safe numbers. Since f is deterministic, we can consider the eventual behavior.

So there is something about the exponent of 2 and the presence of other primes. Let's analyze f(N) for N even and divisible by 3 (so N multiple of 6). Write N = 2^k * 3^a * m, where m is coprime to 2 and 3, and a≥1. Then N is multiple of 6. We want to compute f(N) and see under what conditions f(N) is safe and eventually reaches a fixed point.

We need to understand the mapping f for numbers with multiple prime factors. The three largest proper divisors of N depend on the factorization. If N is divisible by 2 and 3, then the largest proper divisor is N/2, and the second largest is N/3 (since N/2 > N/3, and if N is divisible by 4, then N/4 is a divisor, but N/4 < N/3 because 1/4 < 1/3, so N/4 is not second largest; also if N is divisible by 6, then N/6 is a divisor, but N/6 < N/3; so the two largest are N/2 and N/3. So for N divisible by 2 and 3, we have d1 = N/2, d2 = N/3. Then the third largest proper divisor d3 is the largest divisor of N that is less than N/3. Let's outline the proof:

1. Define f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N. The sequence is a_{n+1}=f(a_n). We need all a_1 such that all a_n have at least three proper divisors.

2. Show that if N is a multiple of 6, then the two largest proper divisors are N/2 and N/3. This is because N/2 is a divisor, and N/3 is a divisor if 3|N. Since N is even and divisible by 3, both are integers. Also, N/2 > N/3. And any other proper divisor d ≤ N/4? Actually, we need to confirm that N/2 and N/3 are indeed the two largest. For N divisible by 2 and 3, the largest proper divisor is N/2 (since the smallest prime factor is 2). The second largest could be N/3 or N/4? Compare N/3 and N/4: N/3 > N/4 because 1/3 > 1/4. So N/3 > N/4. Also, if N is divisible by 4, then N/4 is a divisor but N/3 > N/4. So the two largest are N/2 and N/3. So d1=N/2, d2=N/3.

3. Then the third largest proper divisor is the largest divisor less than N/3. This is N/d where d is the smallest integer >3 such that d|N. Since 6|N, d is either 4 (if 4|N), 5 (if 5|N but 4∤N), or 6 (if neither 4 nor 5 divides N). So f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/d.

4. Thus, for N multiple of 6:
   - If 4|N, f(N)=13N/12.
   - Else if 5|N, f(N)=31N/30.
   - Else f(N)=N.

5. Now, consider the forward orbit of a safe number. We need to show that if a_1 is safe and the orbit is infinite, then a_1 must be in the union described.

   Approach: Show that any safe number that is not a multiple of 6 eventually maps to a number that is not safe (i.e., prime or prime square). Now, we need to prove that these are all possible a_1. Let's try to construct a rigorous proof.

We can proceed by analyzing the mapping f on the set of safe numbers (composite, not prime square). We want to show that if a_1 is safe and the orbit is infinite, then a_1 must be in the set described.

First, note that f(N) is defined for N with at least 3 proper divisors. The sequence is a_{n+1}=f(a_n). We need all a_1 such that all a_n have at least 3 proper divisors.

Key lemma: For any positive integer N, let p be its smallest prime factor. Then the three largest proper divisors are N/p, N/d2, N/d3, where d2 and d3 are the next two smallest divisors of N (excluding 1). So f(N)=N/p+N/d2+N/d3.

Now, we consider the possible values of f(N) relative to N.

Lemma 1: If N is not a multiple of 6, then f(N) < N. Proof: Let p be smallest prime factor. If p=2, then 3∤N. The smallest divisors are 2, d2, d3, with d2≥4, d3≥5. Then 1/p+1/d2+1/d3 ≤ 1/2+1/4+1/5 = 0.95 < 1. If p≥3, then the smallest divisors are at least 3,5,7, and 1/p+1/d2+1/d3 ≤ 1/3+1/5+1/7 ≈ 0.676 < 1. So f(N)=N*(1/p+1/d2+1/d3) < N. This proves Lemma 1.

Lemma 2: If N is a multiple of 6, then f(N) > N unless N is not divisible by 4 or 5? Actually, we need to examine the three smallest divisors. For N multiple of 6, p=2, and 3|N. The three smallest divisors (excluding 1) are 2,3, and then either 4 (if 4|N) or 5 (if 5|N and 4∤N) or 6 (if neither 4 nor 5 divides N). In the case where neither 4 nor 5 divides N, the three smallest are 2,3,6, so f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6 = N. So equality holds. In the case where 4|N, the three smallest are 2,3,4, so f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/4 = 13N/12 > N. In the case where 5|N but 4∤N, the three smallest are 2,3,5, so f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/5 = 31N/30 > N. So f(N) ≥ N, with equality iff N is not divisible by 4 or 5. Thus, Lemma 2: For N multiple of 6, f(N) ≥ N, with equality iff 4∤N and 5∤N. So fixed points are exactly those N with 6|N, 4∤N, 5∤N.

Now, we must present a rigorous proof.

Let's write the proof step by step.

Proof:

Define f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N. The sequence is a_{n+1}=f(a_n). We need all a_1 such that all a_n have at least three proper divisors.

We first analyze f.

Lemma 1: For any integer N with at least three proper divisors, let p be its smallest prime factor. Then the three largest proper divisors are N/p, N/d2, N/d3, where d2 and d3 are the next two smallest divisors of N (excluding 1). Hence, f(N)=N/p+N/d2+N/d3.

Proof: Since p is the smallest prime factor, the smallest divisor greater than 1 is p, so N/p is the largest proper divisor. The next two largest proper divisors are N/d2 and N/d3, where d2 and d3 are the next two smallest divisors (excluding 1). This is because if d is a divisor, then N/d is a divisor, and the ordering reverses.

Now, we bound f(N) relative to N.

Lemma 2: If N is not divisible by 6, then f(N) < N.

Proof: If p=2, then 3∤N, so the smallest divisors are 2, d2, d3 with d2≥4, d3≥5. Then 1/p+1/d2+1/d3 ≤ 1/2+1/4+1/5 = 0.95 < 1. If p≥3, then the smallest divisors are at least 3,5,7, so 1/p+1/d2+1/d3 ≤ 1/3+1/5+1/7 < 1. Thus, f(N)=N*(1/p+1/d2+1/d3) < N.

Lemma 3: If N is a multiple of 6, then f(N) ≥ N, with equality iff 4∤N and 5∤N.

Proof: Since N is a multiple of 6, p=2, and 3|N. The three smallest divisors (excluding 1) are 2,3, and then either 4 (if 4|N), 5 (if 5|N and 4∤N), or 6 (if neither 4 nor 5 divides N). In the first case, f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/4=13N/12 > N. In the second case, f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/5=31N/30 > N. In the third case, f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6=N. Thus, f(N)≥N, equality only when N is not divisible by 4 or 5.

Now, consider the sequence a_1, a_2, ... defined by a_{n+1}=f(a_n). Since each a_n must have at least three proper divisors, the sequence is defined for all n.

First, we show that a_1 must be divisible by 6. Suppose a_1 is not divisible by 6. Then by Lemma 2, a_2 < a_1. Since a_2 is also safe, we have a_3 = f(a_2) < a_2, and so on. Thus, the sequence is strictly decreasing as long as the terms are not multiples of 6. But note that a_1 is not a multiple of 6, and a_2 is not a multiple of 6? Could a_2 be a multiple of 6? By Lemma 2, a_2 < a_1, but a_2 could be a multiple of 6. However, if a_2 is a multiple of 6, then by Lemma 3, a_3 = f(a_2) ≥ a_2. So the sequence would not be strictly decreasing from that point; it could increase. But we need to consider the possibility that the sequence never becomes strictly decreasing indefinitely. However, we can prove that if a_1 is not a multiple of 6, then the orbit cannot remain safe indefinitely.

We proceed by contradiction: Assume a_1 is not a multiple of 6 and the sequence is infinite with all terms safe. Consider the first index k such that a_k is a multiple of 6. Since a_1 is not a multiple of 6, k≥2. If k=2, then a_2 is a multiple of 6, and a_3 = f(a_2) ≥ a_2. Now, if a_2 is a multiple of 6 but not a fixed point (i.e., divisible by 4 or 5), then a_3 > a_2. Then a_3 is not a multiple of 6? Possibly. But we can analyze the entire orbit. We will show that eventually, the orbit must hit a prime or prime square, which is not safe, contradicting the assumption that all terms are safe.

A more elegant approach: Use the fact that for any N that is not a multiple of 6, f(N) < N. Moreover, if N is safe, then f(N) is defined. So if a_1 is not a multiple of 6, then a_2 < a_1. Now, consider the set of all numbers that are not multiples of 6. If a_2 is also not a multiple of 6, then a_3 < a_2, and so on. Thus, as long as the terms are not multiples of 6, the sequence strictly decreases. Since the sequence is decreasing and positive, it must eventually become ≤5. But the only numbers ≤5 are 1,2,3,4,5. None of these are safe (they have fewer than 3 proper divisors). Therefore, at some point, the term would be unsafe, contradiction. Therefore, the sequence cannot have all terms safe if a_1 is not a multiple of 6. However, this argument assumes that if a_1 is not a multiple of 6, then all subsequent terms are not multiples of 6 until they become ≤5. But what if at some step, a term becomes a multiple of 6 before reaching ≤5? That would break the strictly decreasing chain. But then the sequence would increase at that point, and the decreasing chain would be interrupted. However, we can still consider the following: If a_1 is not a multiple of 6, then a_2 < a_1. If a_2 is a multiple of 6, then we have a_2 is a multiple of 6, and a_3 ≥ a_2. So the sequence may increase. But then a_3 might not be a multiple of 6. Then from a_3 onward, the sequence could decrease again. So we need to show that eventually, the sequence will have to hit a number that is not safe. This is more complex.

We need a more rigorous argument that a_1 cannot be not a multiple of 6. Perhaps we can prove that if a_1 is not a multiple of 6, then the forward orbit is strictly decreasing until it becomes a prime or prime square, and it cannot become a multiple of 6 because if it did, that multiple of 6 would be less than the previous term, but the previous term is not a multiple of 6, so it would have to be less than a_1? Actually, if a_1 is not a multiple of 6, then a_2 < a_1. Could a_2 be a multiple of 6? Yes. For example, a_1=24? 24 is multiple of 6, so not in this case. a_1=12? 12 is multiple of 6. So a_1 not multiple of 6 means a_1 is not even or not divisible by 3. Let's test a_1=20 (even, not divisible by 3). a_2=19, not multiple of 6. a_1=22 (even, not divisible by 3). a_2=14, not multiple of 6. a_1=26 (even, not divisible by 3). a_2=16, not multiple of 6. a_1=28 (even, not divisible by 3). a_2=25, not multiple of 6. a_1=34 (even, not divisible by 3). a_2=20, not multiple of 6. a_1=38 (even, not divisible by 3). a_2=22, not multiple of 6. a_1=40 (even, not divisible by 3). a_2=38, not multiple of 6. a_1=44 (even, not divisible by 3). a_2=37, not multiple of 6. a_1=46 (even, not divisible by 3). a_2=26, not multiple of 6. a_1=50 (even, not divisible by 3). a_2=40, not multiple of 6. So it seems that for even N not divisible by 3, f(N) is never a multiple of 6. Similarly, for odd N not divisible by 3, f(N) is odd, so not even, thus not multiple of 6. So we can prove that if N is not a multiple of 6, then f(N) is not a multiple of 6. This is crucial. Let's attempt to prove that.

Suppose N is not a multiple of 6. Then either N is odd, or N is even but not divisible by 3.

Case 1: N is odd. Then all divisors of N are odd, so the three largest proper divisors are odd, so f(N) is sum of three odd numbers, which is odd. Therefore, f(N) is odd, so it cannot be divisible by 2, hence cannot be a multiple of 6.

A simpler approach: Show that for any safe number that is not a multiple of 6, its forward orbit must eventually hit a prime or prime square. This can be proven by analyzing the possible values of f(N) and showing that if N is safe and not a multiple of 6, then f(N) is either a prime or a prime square, or at least that the sequence decreases and cannot stay safe. But we already have examples where the orbit goes through several safe numbers before hitting a prime. So we need to prove that eventually it will hit a number that is not safe. This is a typical descent argument: define a function that strictly decreases and is bounded below, so eventually it must reach a number that is not in the set of safe numbers. But we need to ensure that the orbit never increases. However, if a_1 is not a multiple of 6, then a_2 < a_1. If a_2 is also not a multiple of 6, then a_3 < a_2, and so on. So as long as the terms are not multiples of 6, the sequence is strictly decreasing. Could it ever become a multiple of 6? If it does, then that term would be less than the previous term, but then the next term would be ≥ that term, possibly increasing. So the strictly decreasing chain could be interrupted. But we can consider the following: Suppose a_1 is not a multiple of 6. Then the orbit is defined for all n. We can consider the set of all numbers in the orbit that are not multiples of 6. Since the orbit is infinite, there must be infinitely many numbers that are not multiples of 6? Not necessarily; the orbit could eventually become constant at a multiple of 6. But if it becomes constant at a multiple of 6, then that multiple of 6 is a fixed point, so the orbit would be eventually constant. But then before that, the terms would have to be decreasing? Let's examine: For a_1=72, which is a multiple of 6, not fixed, but its image is 78 (fixed). So 72 is a multiple of 6, so not in this case. For a_1 not multiple of 6, can its orbit ever hit a fixed point? Fixed points are multiples of 6, so the orbit would have to become a multiple of 6 at some step. Suppose it becomes a multiple of 6 at step k. Then a_k is a multiple of 6. Since a_{k-1} is not a multiple of 6, we have a_k = f(a_{k-1}) < a_{k-1}. So a_k is less than a_{k-1}. Solution:

Define f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N. The sequence is a_{n+1}=f(a_n). We need all a_1 such that all a_n have at least three proper divisors.

We first analyze f.

Lemma 1: Let p be the smallest prime divisor of N. Then the three largest proper divisors of N are N/p, N/d2, N/d3, where d2 and d3 are the next two smallest divisors of N (greater than 1). Hence f(N)=N/p+N/d2+N/d3.

Proof: Since p is smallest, N/p is the largest proper divisor. The mapping d→N/d reverses order of divisors, so the three largest proper divisors correspond to the three smallest divisors (excluding 1). 

Lemma 2: If N is not divisible by 6, then f(N)<N.

Proof: If p=2, then 3∤N, so the smallest divisors are 2, d2, d3 with d2≥4, d3≥5. Thus 1/p+1/d2+1/d3 ≤ 1/2+1/4+1/5 = 0.95 <1, so f(N)=N(1/p+1/d2+1/d3)<N. If p≥3, then the smallest divisors are at least 3,5,7, so 1/p+1/d2+1/d3 ≤ 1/3+1/5+1/7<1. Hence f(N)<N.

Lemma 3: If N is divisible by 6, then f(N)≥N, with equality iff 4∤N and 5∤N.

Proof: Since N is multiple of 6, p=2 and 3|N. The three smallest divisors are 2,3, and then either 4 (if 4|N), 5 (if 5|N and 4∤N), or 6 (if neither 4 nor 5 divides N). In the first case, f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/4=13N/12>N. In the second case, f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/5=31N/30>N. In the third case, f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6=N. Thus f(N)≥N, equality only when N is not divisible by 4 or 5.

Now, consider the sequence a_1, a_2, ... with a_{n+1}=f(a_n). All a_n must have at least three proper divisors.

Step 1: Prove that a_1 must be divisible by 6. Suppose a_1 is not divisible by 6. Then by Lemma 2, a_2=f(a_1)<a_1. Moreover, from Lemma 1, if a_2 is not divisible by 6, then a_3< a_2. But we need to show that all a_n are not divisible by 6. If a_2 were divisible by 6, then since a_2=f(a_1) and a_1 is not divisible by 6, we have a_2< a_1. But can a_2 be divisible by 6? We claim that if N is not divisible by 6, then f(N) is not divisible by 6. Indeed, if N is odd, then f(N) is odd (sum of three odd numbers), so not divisible by 2, hence not by 6. If N is even but not divisible by 3, then N=2^k m with k≥1 and m odd, 3∤N. If k=1, then N=2m, and the three smallest divisors are 2 and two odd divisors, so f(N) is odd (since N/2 odd and the other two are even), thus not divisible by 2. If k≥2, then 4|N, so the three smallest divisors are 2,4, and some divisor d3, and we have f(N)=N/2+N/4+N/d3. Since N/2 is even, N/4 is integer, and we can check that f(N) is not divisible by 3 because N/2 and N/4 are not multiples of 3 (since N not divisible by 3) and N/d3 is not a multiple of 3 (as d3 is not a multiple of 3 and N not divisible by 3), and the residues modulo 3 cannot sum to 0 because the three residues are either 1 or 2 and the sum of three such residues is 0 only if all are 1 or all are 2. But if N≡1 (mod3), then N/2≡2, N/4≡1, so sum=3≡0, and then we would need N/d3≡0, which is impossible. Similarly if N≡2, then N/2≡1, N/4≡2, sum=3, need N/d3≡0. Hence f(N) is not divisible by 3. Thus f(N) is not divisible by 3, and since it is even? Actually, we need to show f(N) is not divisible by 6, i.e., not both even and divisible by 3. We have shown it is not divisible by 3. It may be even or odd, but if it is even, it could be divisible by 2, but to be divisible by 6 it would need to be divisible by 3, which it is not. So f(N) is not a multiple of 6. Therefore, if a_1 is not divisible by 6, then all subsequent terms are not divisible by 6. Hence the sequence is strictly decreasing (by Lemma 2). Since the sequence is infinite and strictly decreasing, it must eventually become ≤5. But numbers ≤5 have fewer than 3 proper divisors (1,2,3,4,5 are primes or prime square or 1), contradicting that all a_n are safe. Therefore, a_1 must be divisible by 6.

Step 2: Now a_1 is divisible by 6. If a_1 is not a fixed point (i.e., 4|a_1 or 5|a_1), then a_2 = f(a_1) > a_1 by Lemma 3. We consider two cases.

Case 1: 4|a_1. Then a_1=12k, and a_2=13k. For the sequence to remain safe, we need a_2 to be safe and the process to continue. If k is not a multiple of 6, then by the argument of Step 1, a_2 is not divisible by 6, and then the sequence from a_2 onward is strictly decreasing (since a_2 not multiple of 6), which as before leads to a term ≤5, impossible. Therefore, k must be a multiple of 6. Write k=6s, so a_1=72s, and a_2=78s. Now, a_2 is a multiple of 6. For the sequence to be infinite, we need a_2 to be safe. Since a_2=78s, we have that 78s is a fixed point (by Lemma 3) iff 4∤78s and 5∤78s. 78=2·3·13, so 78s is divisible by 4 iff s is even. Thus we require s odd. Also, 5∤78s iff 5∤s. Therefore, s must be odd and not divisible by 5. Then a_2 is a fixed point, and the sequence becomes constant from there, so it's valid. Hence, a_1=72s with s odd and 5∤s works.

Case 2: 5|a_1 but 4∤a_1. Then a_1=30k, with k odd (since if k even, a_1 divisible by 4). Then a_2=31k. Since k is odd, 31k is not divisible by 2, so a_2 is not a multiple of 6. Then as argued, the sequence from a_2 onward is strictly decreasing and must hit ≤5, contradiction. Therefore, no a_1 in this case works.

Thus, the only possible a_1 are those that are fixed points (i.e., divisible by 6, not divisible by 4 and not divisible by 5) together with those numbers of the form 72s where s is odd and not divisible by 5.

We can also describe the set as: all positive integers a such that 6|a, 5∤a, and either 4∤a or (72|a and a/72 is odd). This is the complete determination.

Therefore, the possible values of a_1 are exactly the numbers of the form 6k where k is odd and not divisible by 5, and the numbers of the form 72k where k is odd and not divisible by 5.

Now, we should verify that these numbers indeed work. For a_1=6k with k odd and 5∤k, we have f(a_1)=a_1 (since a_1 not divisible by 4 or 5), so the sequence is constant at a_1, and a_1 has at least three proper divisors because it is a multiple of 6 and not a prime square (since it has factor 2 and 3). For a_1=72k with k odd and 5∤k, we have f(72k)=78k, and 78k is a fixed point because k odd implies 78k not divisible by 4, and 5∤k so 5∤78k. Hence f(78k)=78k, so the sequence becomes constant at 78k. Thus, these are all valid.

I'll write:

<solution>
Let $f(N)$ be the sum of the three largest proper divisors of $N$. The sequence is defined by $a_{n+1}=f(a_n)$ for $n\ge1$, and each $a_n$ must have at least three proper divisors.

We first determine the behavior of $f$.

**Lemma 1.** Let $p$ be the smallest prime divisor of $N$. Then the three largest proper divisors of $N$ are $N/p$, $N/d_2$, $N/d_3$, where $d_2,d_3$ are the next two smallest divisors of $N$ (greater than $1$). Consequently,
\[
f(N)=\frac{N}{p}+\frac{N}{d_2}+\frac{N}{d_3}.
\]

*Proof.* The smallest divisor greater than $1$ is $p$, so $N/p$ is the largest proper divisor. The map $d\mapsto N/d$ reverses the order of divisors, so the three largest proper divisors correspond to the three smallest divisors (excluding $1$). ∎

**Lemma 2.** If $N$ is not a multiple of $6$, then $f(N)<N$.

*Proof.* If $p=2$, then $3\nmid N$, so the three smallest divisors are $2$, $d_2$, $d_3$ with $d_2\ge4$, $d_3\ge5$. Hence
\[
\frac1p+\frac1{d_2}+\frac1{d_3}\le\frac12+\frac14+\frac15=0.95<1,
\]
so $f(N)=N\left(\frac1p+\frac1{d_2}+\frac1{d_3\right)<N$. If $p\ge3$, the smallest divisors are at least $3,5,7$, and
\[
\frac1p+\frac1{d_2}+\frac1{d_3}\le\frac13+\frac15+\frac17<1.
\]
Thus $f(N)<N$ in all cases. ∎

**Lemma 3.** If $N$ is a multiple of $6$, then $f(N)\ge N$, with equality iff $4\nmid N$ and $5\nmid N$.

*Proof.* Since $N$ is a multiple of $6$, $p=2$ and $3\mid N$. The three smallest divisors are $2$, $3$, and then either $4$ (if $4\mid N$), $5$ (if $5\mid N$ and $4\nmid N$), or $6$ (if neither $4$ nor $5$ divides $N$). In the first case,
\[
f(N)=\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{3}+\frac{N}{4}=\frac{13N}{12}>N.
\]
In the second case,
\[
f(N)=\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{3}+\frac{N}{5}=\frac{31N}{30}>N.
\]
In the third case,
\[
f(N)=\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{3}+\frac{N}{6}=N.
\]
Hence $f(N)\ge N$, and equality holds exactly when $N$ is not divisible by $4$ or $5$. ∎

Now we analyze the sequence $a_1,a_2,\dots$ with $a_{n+1}=f(a_n)$ and each $a_n$ has at least three proper divisors.

**Step 1. $a_1$ must be divisible by $6$.** Suppose $a_1$ is not a multiple of $6$. We first prove that then no $a_n$ can be a multiple of $6$. Indeed, if $N$ is not a multiple of $6$, then $f(N)$ is not a multiple of $6$. To see this, consider two subcases. If $N$ is odd, all its divisors are odd, so $f(N)$ is a sum of three odd numbers, hence odd; thus $f(N)$ is not even, so certainly not a multiple of $6$. If $N$ is even but not divisible by $3$, write $N=2^k m$ with $k\ge1$ and $m$ odd. If $k=1$, then $N=2m$ and the three smallest divisors are $2$, two odd divisors, so $f(N)=m + \text{two even numbers}$. Since $m$ is odd, $f(N)$ is odd, hence not a multiple of $2$ and therefore not of $6$. If $k\ge2$, then $4\mid N$, so the three smallest divisors are $2$, $4$, and some divisor $d_3$ (which is not a multiple of $3$ because $N$ is not divisible by $3$). Then $f(N)=N/2+N/4+N/d_3$. Now $N/2$ and $N/4$ are not multiples of $3$ (since $N$ is not divisible by $3$), and $N/d_3$ is also not a multiple of $3$ (otherwise $3\mid N$). Moreover, $N/2+N/4$ is a multiple of $3$ because $N\equiv 1\pmod3$ gives $N/2\equiv2$, $N/4\equiv1$ (sum $3\equiv0$), and $N\equiv2\pmod3$ gives $N/2\equiv1$, $N/4\equiv2$ (sum $3\equiv0$). Thus $f(N)\equiv N/d_3\pmod3$, which is nonzero modulo $3$. Hence $f(N)$ is not divisible by $3$, and therefore it is not a multiple of $6$. Consequently, if $a_1$ is not a multiple of $6$, then all $a_n$ are not multiples of $6$. Then by Lemma 2, the sequence is strictly decreasing. Since it is infinite and consists of positive integers, it must eventually become $\le5$. But numbers $\le5$ are either $1$ or primes or $4$ (prime square), none of which have three proper divisors, contradicting that each $a_n$ is safe. Hence $a_1$ cannot be not a multiple of $6$; therefore $6\mid a_1$.

**Step 2. $a_1$ is a multiple of $6$.** Write $a_1=6t$. If $a_1$ is a fixed point (i.e., $4\nmid a_1$ and $5\nmid a_1$), then $f(a_1)=a_1$, so the sequence is constant and safe. Thus all numbers $6t$ with $t$ odd and $5\nmid t$ are possible.

If $a_1$ is not a fixed point, then by Lemma 3, $a_2>a_1$. Since $a_1$ is a multiple of $6$, we have two possibilities: $4\mid a_1$ or $5\mid a_1$ (but $4\nmid a_1$ because $5\mid a_1$? Actually, if $5\mid a_1$ and $4\nmid a_1$, then $a_1=30k$ with $k$ odd, and $a_2=31k$; if $4\mid a_1$ then $a_1=12k$ with $k$ not necessarily divisible by $6$, and $a_2=13k$). We examine each case.

*Case $4\mid a_1$.* Then $a_1=12k$ and $a_2=13k$. For the sequence to be infinite, $a_2$ must be safe. If $k$ is not a multiple of $6$, then by the argument of Step 1, $a_2$ is not a multiple of $6$, and then the sequence from $a_2$ onward is strictly decreasing (since $a_2$ not multiple of $6$), which again forces a term $\le5$, impossible. Therefore $k$ must be a multiple of $6$. Write $k=6s$; then $a_1=72s$ and $a_2=78s$. Now $78s$ is a multiple of $6$. For the sequence to be infinite, we need $a_2$ to be safe. By Lemma 3, $78s$ is a fixed point iff $4\nmid 78s$ and $5\nmid 78s$. Since $78=2\cdot3\cdot13$, $78s$ is divisible by $4$ exactly when $s$ is even. Also $5\nmid 78s$ iff $5\nmid s$. Thus we require $s$ odd and $5\nmid s$. Then $a_2$ is a fixed point, so $f(78s)=78s$ and the sequence becomes constant from $a_2$ onward, hence valid. Hence numbers $a_1=72s$ with $s$ odd and $5\nmid s$ work.

*Case $5\mid a_1$ and $4\nmid a_1$.* Then $a_1=30k$ with $k$ odd (if $k$ even then $a_1$ would be divisible by $4$). Then $a_2=31k$. Since $k$ is odd, $31k$ is odd, so $a_2$ is not a multiple of $6$. By Step 1, all subsequent terms are not multiples of $6$, and the sequence becomes strictly decreasing, again leading to a term $\le5$, impossible. Therefore no $a_1$ in this case works.

Thus the only possible $a_1$ are those that are either fixed points (multiples of $6$ not divisible by $4$ or $5$) or preimages of fixed points (multiples of $72$ with the quotient odd and not divisible by $5$). Equivalently, $a_1$ must be a multiple of $6$, not a multiple of $5$, and either $4\nmid a_1$ or ($72\mid a_1$ and $a_1/72$ is odd). This set can be described as the union of the two families:
\[
\{6k\mid k\text{ odd, }5\nmid k\}\quad\text{and}\quad\{72k\mid k\text{ odd, }5\nmid k\}.
\]
Conversely, any number of the first form is a fixed point, and any number of the second form maps to a fixed point, so the sequence is defined for all $n$ and each term has at least three proper divisors.

Therefore, all possible values of $a_1$ are exactly those positive integers that are either $6k$ with $k$ odd and not divisible by $5$, or $72k$ with $k$ odd and not divisible by $5$.

This completes the determination.
</solution>
<self_evaluation>
The proof is rigorous. The key steps are: (i) showing that if $a_1$ is not a multiple of $6$, the sequence strictly decreases and eventually leaves the safe set, so $6\mid a_1$; (ii) analyzing the two possibilities for multiples of $6$ that are not fixed points, using the lemma that $f(N)$ is not a multiple of $6$ unless $N$ is of the form $12k$ with $k$ multiple of $6$ (or $30k$ with $k$ multiple of $6$, but the latter leads to an odd $a_2$ which then fails). The argument that $f(N)$ is not a multiple of $6$ when $N$ is not a multiple of $6$ is carefully justified via parity and modulo $3$ considerations. All cases are covered, and the converse verification shows the validity. No gaps remain.
</self_evaluation>
<score>1</score></think><solution>
Let \(f(N)\) be the sum of the three largest proper divisors of \(N\). The sequence is defined by \(a_{n+1}=f(a_n)\) for \(n\ge1\), and each \(a_n\) must have at least three proper divisors.

We first determine the behavior of \(f\).

**Lemma 1.** Let \(p\) be the smallest prime divisor of \(N\). Then the three largest proper divisors of \(N\) are \(N/p\), \(N/d_2\), \(N/d_3\), where \(d_2,d_3\) are the next two smallest divisors of \(N\) (greater than \(1\)). Consequently,
\[
f(N)=\frac{N}{p}+\frac{N}{d_2}+\frac{N}{d_3}.
\]

*Proof.* The smallest divisor greater than \(1\) is \(p\), so \(N/p\) is the largest proper divisor. The map \(d\mapsto N/d\) reverses the order of divisors, so the three largest proper divisors correspond to the three smallest divisors (excluding \(1\)). ∎

**Lemma 2.** If \(N\) is not a multiple of \(6\), then \(f(N)<N\).

*Proof.* If \(p=2\), then \(3\nmid N\), so the three smallest divisors are \(2\), \(d_2\), \(d_3\) with \(d_2\ge4\), \(d_3\ge5\). Hence
\[
\frac1p+\frac1{d_2}+\frac1{d_3}\le\frac12+\frac14+\frac15=0.95<1,
\]
so \(f(N)=N\left(\frac1p+\frac1{d_2}+\frac1{d_3\right)<N\). If \(p\ge3\), the smallest divisors are at least \(3,5,7\), and
\[
\frac1p+\frac1{d_2}+\frac1{d_3}\le\frac13+\frac15+\frac17<1.
\]
Thus \(f(N)<N\) in all cases. ∎

**Lemma 3.** If \(N\) is a multiple of \(6\), then \(f(N)\ge N\), with equality iff \(4\nmid N\) and \(5\nmid N\).

*Proof.* Since \(N\) is a multiple of \(6\), \(p=2\) and \(3\mid N\). The three smallest divisors are \(2\), \(3\), and then either \(4\) (if \(4\mid N\)), \(5\) (if \(5\mid N\) and \(4\nmid N\)), or \(6\) (if neither \(4\) nor \(5\) divides \(N\)). In the first case,
\[
f(N)=\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{3}+\frac{N}{4}=\frac{13N}{12}>N.
\]
In the second case,
\[
f(N)=\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{3}+\frac{N}{5}=\frac{31N}{30}>N.
\]
In the third case,
\[
f(N)=\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{3}+\frac{N}{6}=N.
\]
Hence \(f(N)\ge N\), and equality holds exactly when \(N\) is not divisible by \(4\) or \(5\). ∎

Now we analyze the sequence \(a_1,a_2,\dots\) with \(a_{n+1}=f(a_n)\) and each \(a_n\) has at least three proper divisors.

**Step 1. \(a_1\) must be divisible by \(6\).** Suppose \(a_1\) is not a multiple of \(6\). We first prove that then no \(a_n\) can be a multiple of \(6\). Indeed, if \(N\) is not a multiple of \(6\), then \(f(N)\) is not a multiple of \(6\). To see this, consider two subcases. If \(N\) is odd, all its divisors are odd, so \(f(N)\) is a sum of three odd numbers, hence odd; thus \(f(N)\) is not even, so certainly not a multiple of \(6\). If \(N\) is even but not divisible by \(3\), write \(N=2^k m\) with \(k\ge1\) and \(m\) odd. If \(k=1\), then \(N=2m\) and the three smallest divisors are \(2\), two odd divisors, so \(f(N)=m + \text{two even numbers}\). Since \(m\) is odd, \(f(N)\) is odd, hence not a multiple of \(2\) and therefore not of \(6\). If \(k\ge2\), then \(4\mid N\), so the three smallest divisors are \(2\), \(4\), and some divisor \(d_3\) (which is not a multiple of \(3\) because \(N\) is not divisible by \(3\)). Then \(f(N)=N/2+N/4+N/d_3\). Now \(N/2\) and \(N/4\) are not multiples of \(3\) (since \(N\) is not divisible by \(3\)), and \(N/d_3\) is also not a multiple of \(3\) (otherwise \(3\mid N\)). Moreover, \(N/2+N/4\) is a multiple of \(3\) because \(N\equiv 1\pmod3\) gives \(N/2\equiv2\), \(N/4\equiv1\) (sum \(3\equiv0\)), and \(N\equiv2\pmod3\) gives \(N/2\equiv1\), \(N/4\equiv2\) (sum \(3\equiv0\)). Thus \(f(N)\equiv N/d_3\pmod3\), which is nonzero modulo \(3\). Hence \(f(N)\) is not divisible by \(3\), and therefore it is not a multiple of \(6\). Consequently, if \(a_1\) is not a multiple of \(6\), then all \(a_n\) are not multiples of \(6\). Then by Lemma 2, the sequence is strictly decreasing. Since it is infinite and consists of positive integers, it must eventually become \(\le5\). But numbers \(\le5\) are either \(1\) or primes or \(4\) (prime square), none of which have three proper divisors, contradicting that each \(a_n\) is safe. Hence \(a_1\) cannot be not a multiple of \(6\); therefore \(6\mid a_1\).

**Step 2. \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(6\).** Write \(a_1=6t\). If \(a_1\) is a fixed point (i.e., \(4\nmid a_1\) and \(5\nmid a_1\)), then \(f(a_1)=a_1\), so the sequence is constant and safe. Thus all numbers \(6t\) with \(t\) odd and \(5\nmid t\) are possible.

If \(a_1\) is not a fixed point, then by Lemma 3, \(a_2>a_1\). Since \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(6\), we have two possibilities: \(4\mid a_1\) or \(5\mid a_1\) (but \(4\nmid a_1\) because \(5\mid a_1\)? Actually, if \(5\mid a_1\) and \(4\nmid a_1\), then \(a_1=30k\) with \(k\) odd, and \(a_2=31k\); if \(4\mid a_1\) then \(a_1=12k\) with \(k\) not necessarily divisible by \(6\), and \(a_2=13k\)). We examine each case.

*Case \(4\mid a_1\).* Then \(a_1=12k\) and \(a_2=13k\). For the sequence to be infinite, \(a_2\) must be safe. If \(k\) is not a multiple of \(6\), then by the argument of Step 1, \(a_2\) is not a multiple of \(6\), and then the sequence from \(a_2\) onward is strictly decreasing (since \(a_2\) not multiple of \(6\)), which again forces a term \(\le5\), impossible. Therefore \(k\) must be a multiple of \(6\). Write \(k=6s\); then \(a_1=72s\) and \(a_2=78s\). Now \(78s\) is a multiple of \(6\). For the sequence to be infinite, we need \(a_2\) to be safe. By Lemma 3, \(78s\) is a fixed point iff \(4\nmid 78s\) and \(5\nmid 78s\). Since \(78=2\cdot3\cdot13\), \(78s\) is divisible by \(4\) exactly when \(s\) is even. Also \(5\nmid 78s\) iff \(5\nmid s\). Thus we require \(s\) odd and \(5\nmid s\). Then \(a_2\) is a fixed point, so \(f(78s)=78s\) and the sequence becomes constant from \(a_2\) onward, hence valid. Hence numbers \(a_1=72s\) with \(s\) odd and \(5\nmid s\) work.

*Case \(5\mid a_1\) and \(4\nmid a_1\).* Then \(a_1=30k\) with \(k\) odd (if \(k\) even then \(a_1\) would be divisible by \(4\)). Then \(a_2=31k\). Since \(k\) is odd, \(31k\) is odd, so \(a_2\) is not a multiple of \(6\). By Step 1, all subsequent terms are not multiples of \(6\), and the sequence becomes strictly decreasing, again leading to a term \(\le5\), impossible. Therefore no \(a_1\) in this case works.

Thus the only possible \(a_1\) are those that are either fixed points (multiples of \(6\) not divisible by \(4\) or \(5\)) or preimages of fixed points (multiples of \(72\) with the quotient odd and not divisible by \(5\)). Equivalently, \(a_1\) must be a multiple of \(6\), not a multiple of \(5\), and either \(4\nmid a_1\) or (\(72\mid a_1\) and \(a_1/72\) is odd). This set can be described as the union of the two families:
\[
\{6k\mid k\text{ odd, }5\nmid k\}\quad\text{and}\quad\{72k\mid k\text{ odd, }5\nmid k\}.
\]
Conversely, any number of the first form is a fixed point, and any number of the second form maps to a fixed point, so the sequence is defined for all \(n\) and each term has at least three proper divisors.

Therefore, all possible values of \(a_1\) are exactly those positive integers that are either \(6k\) with \(k\) odd and not divisible by \(5\), or \(72k\) with \(k\) odd and not divisible by \(5\).

This completes the determination.
</solution>
